In other words, the sequence is infinite iff the iterated S eventually lands on a number N such that S(N)=N (self-sum). Given the recursion, we might be able to prove that if a number is not divisible by 5, then the sequence eventually becomes constant? But 12 is not divisible by 5, yet it leads to 13 prime, which fails. So not just 5. Perhaps we need to consider the behavior of the 2-adic valuation and 3-adic valuation. Let's analyze the transformation in terms of prime factorization.

Let N be composite with at least 3 proper divisors. Let p be the smallest prime factor. Then d1 = p. Now, the second smallest divisor >1, d2, could be either p^2 if p^2 divides N, or the next prime factor q if p^2 does not divide N and q < p^2? Actually careful: The divisors >1 are all numbers that divide N. The smallest is p. The next smallest could be either p^2 if p^2 divides N and p^2 < q? But if p^2 divides N, then p^2 is a divisor. Also, if N has another prime factor q > p, then q is also a divisor. Since p^2 may be less than q or greater? For p=2, p^2=4. For N with prime factors 2 and 3, we have divisors:2,3,4,... If 4 divides N, then 4 is a divisor. So the second smallest is min(p^2, q). Since p^2 is often smaller than q for small p? For p=2, p^2=4, and if q=3, then 3 < 4, so the second smallest is 3, not 4. So the ordering depends on the actual values. For p=3, p^2=9. If N has a prime factor q=5, then 5<9, so second smallest is 5. So the second smallest divisor >1 is the smallest number among p^2 and the next prime factor q (and also possibly p^3 if p^3 divides N? But p^3 would be even larger). So generally, the three smallest divisors >1 are: p, then the smallest among the set {p^2, q, ...} where q is the second smallest prime factor, then the next smallest among the remaining divisors. So it's a bit complex.

Maybe we can find a different approach. Consider the function f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. We can try to find invariant or monotonic quantity. Perhaps we can consider the sum of all proper divisors? Or the sum of the three largest is always at least something. Another idea: For any composite N, the three largest proper divisors are N/p, N/q, N/r where p,q,r are the three smallest divisors >1. Note that p, q, r are all at most N/2? Actually p is at most N/2. q is at most N/3? Not necessarily; e.g., N=2*3*5=30, p=2, q=3, r=5, so q=N/10? Actually N/3=10, q=3 is less than 10. So q is usually less than N/3? Not sure. But we have that p, q, r are divisors, and they are the smallest three. So they are relatively small. Then S(N) = N*(1/p+1/q+1/r). Since p,q,r are small, the sum of reciprocals is often >1? For (2,3,6) sum=1, (2,4,4) sum=1, (3,3,3) sum=1, but (2,3,5) sum=1/2+1/3+1/5=31/30>1, so S(N)>N. (2,4,5) sum=1/2+1/4+1/5=0.5+0.25+0.2=0.95<1, S(N)<N. So the transformation can increase or decrease.

Given that the sequence is infinite, we need to avoid hitting a number with <3 proper divisors. Those numbers are: primes (only 1 proper divisor), squares of primes (only 2 proper divisors), and maybe also numbers like 2^2=4? That's square of prime, proper divisors:1,2 only two. So numbers with exactly 2 proper divisors are of the form p^2. Also numbers with exactly 1 proper divisor are primes. So we need to avoid reaching a prime or a square of a prime. Also numbers with 3 proper divisors? That would be numbers with exactly 4 divisors total, i.e., numbers of the form p^3 or p*q where p and q are distinct primes. For p^3, divisors:1,p,p^2,p^3; proper:1,p,p^2 (three proper divisors). For p*q, divisors:1,p,q,pq; proper:1,p,q (three proper divisors). So numbers with exactly three proper divisors are allowed. So we need to avoid primes and squares of primes.

Now, note that if a_n is a self-sum number, then a_{n+1}=a_n, constant, and since self-sum numbers have at least three proper divisors (they are composite with at least 4 divisors), the sequence is infinite. So self-sum numbers are possible a_1. Also, numbers that eventually map to a self-sum number under iteration of S are possible, provided that all intermediate terms have at least three proper divisors. So we need to find all numbers that under repeated application of S eventually become a fixed point (self-sum). And also we need to ensure that the sequence never hits a prime or square of a prime before reaching the fixed point. But if it hits a prime or square of a prime, then the next term is not defined (or the term itself fails condition because it has <3 proper divisors). So we need to exclude starting numbers that eventually lead to such numbers.

Thus, the problem is to characterize the set of positive integers N such that the orbit under S never contains a prime or a prime square, and also the orbit is infinite. Since the orbit is infinite, it must eventually become periodic. If it becomes periodic, the period must be a cycle. We need to find all cycles. From our experiments, we have seen that S(N)=N for many N (self-sum numbers). Are there any cycles of length >1? Let's try to find a possible cycle of length 2. Suppose S(N)=M and S(M)=N, with N≠M. Then both N and M have at least 3 proper divisors. Also, S(N) = N*(1/p+1/q+1/r) = M, and S(M)=M*(1/p'+1/q'+1/r') = N. Then product S(N)*S(M) = N*M*(1/p+1/q+1/r)(1/p'+1/q'+1/r') = N*M. So (1/p+1/q+1/r)(1/p'+1/q'+1/r') = 1. Since both sums are rational numbers, we need to see if there is a solution with N and M not equal to themselves. Possibly (1/2+1/3+1/6)=1, so if one sum equals 1, then the other must also equal 1. So if both sums equal 1, then S(N)=N and S(M)=M, so they are fixed points, not a 2-cycle. So for a 2-cycle, we need that the two sums are reciprocals? Actually product =1, so if one sum is >1, the other must be <1. So we need a pair of triples (p,q,r) and (p',q',r') such that (1/p+1/q+1/r) * (1/p'+1/q'+1/r') = 1, and also S(N)=M and S(M)=N. But note that N = M*(1/p'+1/q'+1/r')? Wait S(M)=M*(1/p'+1/q'+1/r') = N, so N = M * s', where s' = 1/p'+1/q'+1/r'. And M = N * s, where s = 1/p+1/q+1/r. Then N = N * s * s' => s * s' = 1. So indeed product =1. So we need two rational numbers s and s' with s*s'=1, and s = 1/p+1/q+1/r, s' = 1/p'+1/q'+1/r'. Also, N and M are integers: N = M s', M = N s. So N = N s s' = N, okay. But we also need that the three smallest divisors of N are exactly p,q,r, and of M are exactly p',q',r'. And N and M must be such that the transformation S yields the other. So we need to find if there exists a pair of numbers (N,M) with S(N)=M and S(M)=N, N≠M.

Let's try to search for small numbers. We already computed S for numbers up to, say, 100. Let's list S(N) for N with at least 3 proper divisors, and see if any number maps to another that maps back. We have:

We need to determine all possible a_1. The condition for infinite sequence is that the orbit never hits a prime or a prime square. From our empirical data, many numbers eventually hit a prime or a prime square, but some numbers become constant at a self-sum number. Also, some numbers might eventually become periodic in a cycle that avoids primes. We have not found any cycle other than fixed points. Could there be a cycle of length >1? Let's try to search for possible cycles. Suppose S(N)=M and S(M)=N, with N≠M. Then we have N = M * s' and M = N * s, where s = 1/p+1/q+1/r and s' = 1/p'+1/q'+1/r'. Then s * s' = 1. Since s and s' are rational numbers with numerator and denominator positive integers. Also, N and M are integers. From s*s'=1, we have s and s' are reciprocals. Since s = 1/p+1/q+1/r, with p,q,r divisors of N, and similarly for M. We can try to see if there is any solution. For small s, s can be less than 1, but s' would be >1. s' = 1/p'+1/q'+1/r' >1, which is possible only if the triple is (2,3,6) giving s=1, or (2,4,4) giving s=1, or (3,3,3) giving s=1. So s'=1 would require s=1. So for s>1, s'<1. But s>1 is possible only if the triple is (2,3,5) giving s=31/30>1, (2,3,4) giving s=13/12>1, (2,3,3) giving s=1/2+1/3+1/3=7/6>1, etc. For s>1, s'<1, and s' = 1/p'+1/q'+1/r' <1. So p',q',r' are at least 2, and the sum of reciprocals is less than 1. That is possible for many triples. But we also have the integer constraints. For a cycle, N = M*s' and M = N*s, so N = N*s*s' => s*s'=1. So s and s' are reciprocals. So if s>1, s'<1. So we need two rational numbers that are reciprocals, each of the form sum of reciprocals of three positive integers (with the condition that they are the three smallest divisors of some integer). It might be possible. For example, take s = 1/2+1/3+1/5 = 31/30, then s' = 30/31, which is not of the form sum of reciprocals of three integers? Possibly 30/31 = 1/p+1/q+1/r. This would require p,q,r > 1, and the sum of reciprocals is less than 1. Could we have 1/2+1/3+1/?? 1/2+1/3=5/6, need 1/6? That would be 1/2+1/3+1/6=1, not 30/31. 1/2+1/4+1/?? = 3/4, need 1/4? That gives 1. So maybe not. But we can try to solve 1/p+1/q+1/r = 30/31. Since 30/31 is close to 1, the triple must consist of small numbers. The largest possible sum with p,q,r >=2 is 1/2+1/3+1/6=1, but that's 1, not 30/31. The next largest sum with distinct numbers? 1/2+1/3+1/7 = 41/42 ≈0.976, which is >30/31? 41/42 ≈0.976, 30/31≈0.9677, so 41/42 is larger. 1/2+1/3+1/8 = 23/24≈0.9583, smaller. So maybe no triple gives exactly 30/31. But we don't know. However, we also have the condition that p,q,r are the three smallest divisors of N. So N must be divisible by 2,3,5? For s=31/30, N would have smallest divisors 2,3,5? But then the sum of reciprocals of 2,3,5 is 31/30 >1, so S(N) = N*(31/30) > N. So M = S(N) > N. Then M has smallest divisors p',q',r' such that sum = 30/31 <1. So M must have smallest divisors that are larger than 2,3,5? Possibly 2,3,? But if M has a divisor 2, then 1/2 is part of the sum. The sum of reciprocals of three divisors of M, with smallest divisors, would include 1/2. Then the sum would be at least 1/2+1/3+1/4=13/12>1, which is >30/31. So M cannot have 2 as a divisor if the sum is <1. So M must be odd. Then the smallest divisor is at least 3. So p'>=3, q'>=3, r'>=3. The maximum sum with p',q',r' >=3 is 1/3+1/3+1/3=1, so it's possible to get sum less than 1, e.g., 1/3+1/3+1/4=11/12≈0.9167, but we need exactly 30/31≈0.9677. Could there be a triple of odd numbers? 1/3+1/3+1/5=31/45≈0.6889, too small. 1/3+1/5+1/7=71/105≈0.676, too small. 1/3+1/3+1/6=1, but 6 is even, so not all odd. So maybe no solution. But we need a rigorous argument.

Given the problem is from an olympiad, it's likely that the only infinite sequences are those that become constant at a self-sum number. And the set of possible a_1 is exactly the set of numbers that eventually reach a self-sum number under the transformation. But we need to find all such a_1. Perhaps there is a characterization: a_1 must be a multiple of 6? But 30 is multiple of 6 and fails. So maybe a_1 must be a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5? But 12 is multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, yet fails. So not.

Alternatively, perhaps the only possible a_1 are those that are themselves self-sum numbers (i.e., fixed points). But we saw 72 works, which is not self-sum. So that's not.

Maybe there is a classification: All numbers that are not divisible by 5? But 12 is not divisible by 5, fails. So no.

Let's think more systematically. The transformation S(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. For a given N, let its divisors in increasing order: 1 = d0 < d1 < d2 < ... < d_k = N. Then S(N) = d_{k-1} + d_{k-2} + d_{k-3}. Note that d_{k-1} = N/p, where p = d1 is the smallest prime factor. d_{k-2} = N/q, where q is the second smallest divisor >1? Actually d_{k-2} is the second largest proper divisor, which corresponds to N divided by the second smallest divisor >1? Not exactly: Since divisors come in pairs (d, N/d), the three largest proper divisors are N/d1, N/d2, N/d3. So S(N) = N/d1 + N/d2 + N/d3.

Now, note that d1, d2, d3 are the three smallest divisors >1. They are at least 2, and they are divisors of N. So we can think of the transformation as: S(N) = N * (1/d1 + 1/d2 + 1/d3). Since d1,d2,d3 are divisors, we have d1 * d_{k-1} = N, etc.

Now, we are interested in the orbit under S. Since S(N) can be greater or less than N, the orbit might increase or decrease. But we need to avoid primes and prime squares.

Observation: For any composite N, the sum of the three largest proper divisors is at most N? Not always; 12 gives 13>12. So S(N) can be > N. However, if N is a multiple of 6, then d1=2, d2=3, d3 might be 4 or 5 or 6, etc. If N is divisible by 2,3, and also by 4? Then d1=2, d2=3, d3=4, then S(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = 13N/12 > N. So S(N) > N. If N is divisible by 2,3, and not by 4, then d3 might be 5 or 6, etc. If N is divisible by 2,3, and 5, then d3=5, S(N)=N/2+N/3+N/5 = (31/30)N > N. So S(N) > N. If N is divisible by 2,3, and 6? But 6 is not smaller than 5? Actually if N is divisible by 2,3, and also by 6, then the three smallest divisors are 2,3, and then the next smallest could be 4 if 4 divides N, or 5 if 5 divides N, or 6 if 6 is a divisor and no smaller than 6? But 6 is larger than 4 and 5, so the third smallest is the smallest among 4,5,6, etc. So if N is divisible by 2 and 3 but not by 4 or 5, then the third smallest is 6? But 6 is a divisor, and 6 is larger than 4 and 5. So d3=6. Then S(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6 = N. So S(N)=N. That is the self-sum case. So the fixed points are numbers that are divisible by 2 and 3 but not by 4 or 5. Also, if N is divisible by 2,3, and also by 4, then S(N) > N. If N is divisible by 2,3, and by 5, then S(N) > N. If N is divisible by 2,3, and by 6? But 6 is already a divisor, but if N is divisible by 6, then the third smallest is the smallest among 4,5,6. Since 6 is present, but if 4 and 5 are not divisors, then d3=6. So S(N)=N. So numbers divisible by 6 but not by 4 or 5 are fixed points. But also numbers divisible by 2 and 3 but not by 4 or 5: that is the same as numbers with v2=1 and v5=0, and v3>=1. So these are the self-sum numbers.

Now, what about numbers that are not divisible by 2 or 3? For example, N=25 (5^2) gives proper divisors 1,5; only two, so condition fails. N=49 gives 1,7; fails. So any prime square fails. So if a number is a prime square, it has only two proper divisors, so cannot be in the sequence. So we must avoid those. Also, primes themselves have only one proper divisor, so not allowed.

Now, consider numbers that are multiples of 2 but not 3? For example, N=8 gives S=7, prime. N=10 gives S=8, then 7 prime. N=14 gives S=10, then 8, then 7. So many lead to prime. Could there be a cycle that avoids primes? Possibly numbers like 2*prime? For N=2p, where p is an odd prime > 2, then divisors:1,2,p,2p. So three largest proper: p,2,1? Actually proper divisors:1,2,p. The three largest are p,2,1. So S= p+2+1 = p+3. That is > N? Our analysis shows that many numbers eventually lead to a prime or prime square, but some numbers lead to a fixed point. We have seen that numbers like 72, 216, 504, 648, 744? Wait 744? Let's check 744: 744=2^3*3*31, smallest:2,3,4? 4 divides 744? 744/4=186, yes. So S=372+248+186=806, 806=2*13*31, then 403+? Actually 806: smallest:2,13,31? Then S=403+26+? Let's compute: 806=2*13*31, divisors:1,2,13,26,31,62,403,806. Three largest:403,62,31 sum=496, then 496 leads to 281? Actually 496=2^4*31, then S=248+124+62=434, then 434 leads to 370? This seems to lead to failure. So not all multiples of 6 lead to fixed points. Those that are self-sum are fixed points. But numbers like 72 lead to 78, which is a fixed point. So they are not fixed themselves but map to a fixed point.

We need to determine all a_1 such that the orbit does not hit a prime or prime square. The fixed points are the self-sum numbers, and they are the only numbers that are constant. Also, numbers that map to a fixed point after some steps are valid. So the set of valid a_1 is the set of numbers that eventually reach a self-sum number under iteration of S. But we also must consider if there are any cycles that avoid primes, but we have not found any. Could there be a cycle of length 2? Let's try to see if any number N such that S(N)=M and S(M)=N, with N≠M. We can attempt to find such cycles. Suppose s = 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3 > 1, so N is not self-sum. Then M = N*s > N. Then M has smallest divisors p',q',r' such that sum = 1/s'? Actually s' = 1/p'+1/q'+1/r' = 1/s < 1, so M is odd and has no divisor 2. Then the sum of reciprocals of its three smallest divisors is less than 1. The maximum such sum is 1/3+1/3+1/3=1, so it's possible. For a cycle, we need N = M * s' and M = N * s. So N = N * s * s' => s*s'=1. So s'=1/s. So s and 1/s are both of the form sum of reciprocals of three positive integers (with the condition that they are the three smallest divisors of some integer). For s > 1, 1/s < 1. So we need to find if there exist integers a,b,c (the three smallest divisors of N) such that 1/a+1/b+1/c = s > 1, and integers a',b',c' (three smallest divisors of M) such that 1/a'+1/b'+1/c' = 1/s < 1. Also, N = M * (1/s) and M = N * s. So N and M are related. But we also have that N is divisible by a,b,c, and M is divisible by a',b',c'. And from the pairing, N and M must be such that these divisor sets are consistent. It might be possible that such a cycle exists. For example, consider s = 1/2+1/3+1/4 = 13/12 ≈1.0833, then 1/s = 12/13 ≈0.9231. Is there a triple of positive integers with sum of reciprocals = 12/13? The maximum sum with integers >=2 is 1/2+1/3+1/6=1, but we need 12/13. Could we have 1/2+1/3+1/7 = 41/42≈0.976, too big. 1/2+1/3+1/8=23/24≈0.958, too small. So no triple gives exactly 12/13. What about s = 1/2+1/3+1/5 = 31/30≈1.0333, then 1/s=30/31≈0.9677. Need triple with sum 30/31. The maximum with distinct numbers? 1/2+1/3+1/6=1, 1/2+1/3+1/7=41/42≈0.976, 1/2+1/3+1/8=23/24≈0.958, so 41/42 is larger, 23/24 is smaller. So no exact. s = 1/2+1/4+1/4 = 1, that's 1, then 1/s=1, which gives s=1, so both are 1. So fixed points. s = 1/2+1/3+1/7 = 41/42, then 1/s=42/41>1, so s' would be >1, but then we need s*s'=1, so s'=1/s=42/41, which is not of the form sum of reciprocals? Could be 1/2+1/3+1/7? That's 41/42, not 42/41. So no. s = 1/2+1/4+1/6 = 11/12≈0.9167, then 1/s=12/11≈1.0909. Need triple with sum 12/11. Could be 1/2+1/3+1/6=1, too small; 1/2+1/3+1/4=13/12≈1.083, close but not exactly; 1/2+1/3+1/5=31/30≈1.033, too small; 1/2+1/4+1/5=19/20=0.95, too small. So no.

It seems unlikely that there is a cycle of length 2. More generally, we can try to prove that if S(N) > N, then S(N) is composite with at least 3 proper divisors? Not always; 12 gives 13 prime. So S(N) can be prime. But maybe we can show that if N is not a fixed point, then S(N) is either prime or leads to a prime quickly. This would classify all possible a_1: they are exactly those numbers that eventually become a fixed point. But we need a rigorous proof.

Let's step back. The problem is to determine all possible values of a_1 such that the infinite sequence exists (each term has at least 3 proper divisors). This is a typical problem where you need to find that the only possible sequences are those that eventually become constant at a number of the form 6t with t odd and not multiple of 5. And then find all starting numbers that lead to such a constant. Often, the answer is that a_1 must be a multiple of 6 that is not divisible by 5? But we saw 12 fails. So maybe a_1 must be a multiple of 6 and also not divisible by 4? Wait 12 is divisible by 4, and fails. 18 is not divisible by 4, works. 24 is divisible by 4, fails. 30 is divisible by 5, fails. 36 is divisible by 4? 36=4*9, so divisible by 4, fails. 42 is not divisible by 4, works. 48 is divisible by 4, fails. 54 is not divisible by 4, works. 60 is divisible by 4 and 5, fails. 66 is not divisible by 4, works. 72 is divisible by 4, but works because it leads to 78 (which is not divisible by 4). So the condition is not simply v2(N)=1. So we need a more subtle invariant.

Observation: The transformation S(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. For any composite N, let its prime factorization. Consider the sum of reciprocals of the three smallest divisors. The transformation essentially multiplies N by the sum of reciprocals. So S(N) = N * (1/d1+1/d2+1/d3). Since d1,d2,d3 are divisors, the product d1*d2*d3 is something. Actually, note that d1, d2, d3 are the three smallest divisors >1. They are not necessarily distinct, but they are divisors. For N, let p = d1 be the smallest prime factor. Then the three smallest divisors are: p, then the smallest among p^2 and the next prime factor q, then the next smallest. So the sum of reciprocals can be 1, 1/p+1/q+1/r, etc.

Now, we need to consider the orbit. Perhaps we can show that if N is not a fixed point, then the sequence eventually decreases. But from our computations, many numbers increase initially (e.g., 12->13, but 13 is prime, so not allowed; 20->19 prime; 24->26 then 16, etc.). So it's not monotonic.

Another approach: Let's try to characterize all numbers that are reachable from a fixed point under the transformation. That is, find all numbers N such that iterating S eventually reaches a fixed point. Since fixed points are self-sum numbers (v2=1, v5=0, v3>=1), we need to find preimages. The transformation is S(N) = N*(1/d1+1/d2+1/d3). So given a fixed point F, we want N such that S(N)=F. That means N*(1/d1+1/d2+1/d3)=F. Since F is self-sum, we have 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3=1. So N= F. So fixed points are the only numbers that map to themselves. But there could be numbers that map to a fixed point in one step: S(N)=F. Then N = F / (1/d1+1/d2+1/d3). Since F is fixed, 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3 is the sum of reciprocals of the three smallest divisors of N. That sum must be less than 1? Actually if S(N)=F, then N = F / s, where s = 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3. Since F is self-sum, and N is an integer, s must divide F? Not necessarily, because N is integer. So we need to find N such that the sum of reciprocals of its three smallest divisors equals F/N. Since F/N is a rational number. This is a kind of Diophantine equation.

Thus, the set of all valid a_1 is the set of numbers that eventually reach a self-sum number under iteration of S. This set includes all numbers that are themselves self-sum (fixed points) and all numbers that are preimages of self-sum numbers under the transformation. Since the transformation is deterministic, we can characterize the preimages. We have seen that if N is composite with at least 3 proper divisors, then S(N) = N * (1/d1+1/d2+1/d3). The sum s = 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3 is a rational number. For a given fixed point F, we want to find N such that S(N)=F. That is N * s = F. So N = F / s. Since s is the sum of reciprocals of three smallest divisors of N, and N is integer, s must be a rational number of the form F/N. So we need to find all N such that the sum of reciprocals of its three smallest divisors equals F/N. This is a kind of inverse problem.

From our computations, the only s that appear for numbers that eventually become fixed are 1 (for fixed points themselves) and 13/12 (for numbers with smallest divisors 2,3,4) and 31/30? Let's define a sequence of families:
- Family F_0: self-sum numbers (fixed points). These are numbers N such that v2(N)=1, v5(N)=0, v3(N)>=1. They satisfy S(N)=N.

Now, we need to present the proof. Let's structure it:

1. Define S(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N. The sequence is a_{n+1}=S(a_n).

2. Condition: Each a_n must have at least 3 proper divisors, so a_n must be composite and not a prime square (since prime square has exactly 2 proper divisors). Also, a_n cannot be a prime (only 1 proper divisor). So a_n must be composite with at least 4 divisors.

3. We analyze the transformation. Let p be the smallest prime factor of N. Then the three smallest divisors >1 are: p, then the smallest among p^2 and the next prime factor q, then the next smallest. In general, if N is divisible by 2, then the smallest divisors are 2,3,4 if v2(N)>=2 and v3(N)>=1; or 2,3,6 if v2(N)=1 and v3(N)>=1; or 2,3,5 if v5(N)>=1 and v2(N)=1 and v3(N)>=1? But 6 is always a divisor if 2 and 3 divide N, so the third smallest cannot be 5 because 6 < 5? Actually 6 > 5, so 5 would be smaller than 6, but 5 must be a divisor, so if 2 and 3 divide N, then 6 divides N. So the third smallest could be 4 if v2>=2, or 5 if v5>=1 and v2=1? But if v2=1 and v3>=1, then 6 divides N, and 4 does not, so the divisors are 2,3,5? But 6 is a divisor and 6 > 5, so the third smallest is 5 if 5 divides N, but 6 is larger. So the triple could be (2,3,5) if v2=1, v3>=1, v5>=1. However, 6 is also a divisor, but 6 > 5, so the three smallest are 2,3,5. So that is possible. For example, N=30=2*3*5 gives triple (2,3,5). So the sum s = 1/2+1/3+1/5 = 31/30. So S(N)=N*31/30. For N=30, S=31 prime. So this case leads to odd image, which may be prime. So it's not allowed.

4. Key observation: For N to have an infinite orbit under S, the orbit must never hit a prime or a prime square. We prove that this forces the orbit to eventually become constant at a fixed point. Moreover, the only fixed points are numbers N such that S(N)=N. We see that numbers like 6,18,42,54,66,78, etc. have the property that the sum of the three largest proper divisors equals the number itself. That is, a_n is a "self-sum" number. Also, numbers like 72 lead to 78 which is self-sum, then constant. Also, 30? 30 gave 31 prime, no. 42 gave 42 constant, so 42 works. 54 works. 66 works. 78 works. What about 90? No. 12? What about numbers like 20? No. So there is a pattern: numbers that are multiples of 6? 6,18,42,54,66,78,... but 30 is multiple of 6 but fails. So which ones? Let's compute the sum of three largest proper divisors for a number N. The three largest proper divisors are N/p, N/q, N/r where p,q,r are the three smallest divisors >1. That sum is N*(1/p+1/q+1/r). For N to be self-sum, we need N = N*(1/p+1/q+1/r) => 1 = 1/p+1/q+1/r. So p,q,r are the three smallest divisors >1 (including possibly repeated powers) such that 1/p+1/q+1/r = 1. This is a known Egyptian fraction problem. The possible triples of distinct positive integers (p,q,r) with p<=q<=r and 1/p+1/q+1/r=1 are: (2,3,6) and (2,4,4) and (3,3,3)? Check: 1/3+1/3+1/3=1, so (3,3,3) works. Also (2,3,6): 1/2+1/3+1/6=1. (2,4,4): 1/2+1/4+1/4=1. Are there any others? (2,3,6) gives sum 1, (2,4,4) gives sum 1, (3,3,3) gives sum 1. Also (2,5,? ) 1/2+1/5=0.7, need 0.3 => 1/3.33 not integer. So only those. Also possibly (2,3,6) with p=2,q=3,r=6; (2,4,4); (3,3,3). Also (2,2,? ) 1/2+1/2=1, so need 0, impossible. So these are the only triples of positive integers (not necessarily distinct) that sum to 1.

Now, the three smallest divisors >1 of N must be exactly these p,q,r. But careful: The three smallest divisors >1 are not necessarily distinct; they could be p, p^2, etc. For (3,3,3), we need the three smallest divisors >1 to be 3,3,3. That means the smallest divisor >1 is 3, and the next two divisors are also 3? But divisors are distinct unless N is a perfect square? Actually divisors are distinct; if 3 is a divisor, it appears only once. So we cannot have three 3's as divisors. The three smallest divisors >1 are distinct numbers. So the triple (3,3,3) cannot occur because the divisors are distinct positive integers. So the only possible triples are (2,3,6) and (2,4,4). But (2,4,4) has two 4's; but divisors are distinct, so we cannot have two 4's unless 4 appears twice? But divisors are distinct, so we cannot have the same divisor listed twice. However, the three smallest divisors >1 are the three smallest numbers among the divisors excluding 1. They are distinct because each divisor is a distinct integer. So if N has divisors 2,4,4? That would mean 4 appears twice, but it's the same number; it's only one divisor. So we cannot have the same number repeated as a divisor. So the triple (2,4,4) would require that the set of divisors includes 2 and 4, and that the next smallest divisor after 2 and 4 is also 4? That's impossible because after 2 and 4, the next smallest is >4 (unless there is another divisor equal to 4, which doesn't exist). So the triple (2,4,4) is not possible as the three smallest divisors >1 because they must be distinct. However, we could have the three smallest divisors >1 as 2,4, and something else. But the condition for self-sum is 1/p+1/q+1/r=1. For (2,4,4), we need three divisors, but the third is also 4, which is not distinct. So we need to reconsider: The three largest proper divisors are d_{k-1}, d_{k-2}, d_{k-3}. They correspond to N/d1, N/d2, N/d3 where d1,d2,d3 are the three smallest divisors >1. But d1,d2,d3 are distinct because they are distinct divisors. So the triple (2,4,4) cannot happen. So only (2,3,6) works? But we also saw numbers like 6,18,42,54,66,78 that are self-sum. For 6, divisors >1: 2,3,6? Actually 6's divisors: 1,2,3,6; so smallest three >1: 2,3,6. That's (2,3,6). For 18, divisors:1,2,3,6,9,18; smallest three >1:2,3,6? Actually 2,3,6 are the smallest three. But 6 is a divisor, and 2,3,6 are the three smallest >1. So (2,3,6). For 66, divisors:1,2,3,6,11,22,33,66; smallest three:2,3,6. So (2,3,6). For 78, divisors:1,2,3,6,13,26,39,78; smallest three:2,3,6. So indeed all self-sum numbers we found have the three smallest divisors >1 as 2,3,6. That implies that N must be divisible by 2,3,6? Actually 6 is a divisor, so N is divisible by 6. More precisely, N must not be divisible by 4, so its 2-adic valuation is exactly 1. That is, v2(N)=1. Also, v5(N)=0. Also, v3(N)>=1, but we need 3 to be a divisor, so v3(N)>=1. So the self-sum numbers are exactly numbers N such that N is divisible by 6 but not by 4 or 5. That is, N = 6 * t, where t is odd and not divisible by 5. Now, for a_1 such that the sequence is infinite, we need that each a_n has at least three proper divisors. The recursion defines a_{n+1} as sum of three largest proper divisors of a_n. So we need to consider the possible values of a_1 that lead to an infinite sequence where each term is composite with at least 3 proper divisors (i.e., has at least 4 divisors). Possibly the sequence might become periodic or constant, and we need to ensure that it never hits a number with fewer than 3 proper divisors. From our tests, many numbers eventually lead to a prime or a number with only 2 proper divisors. The ones that work are those that eventually become self-sum numbers? Actually we saw that if a_1 = 6, then a_2 = 6, constant; if a_1 = 18, then a_2 = 18; if a_1 = 42, then a_2 = 42; etc. Also, a_1 = 72 leads to a_2 = 78, then constant at 78. So 72 works. Also, a_1 = 30? So it seems that many numbers eventually go to a prime or to a number with only two proper divisors. But some numbers like 72 eventually hit a self-sum number, then constant. Also, what about numbers that lead to a self-sum number that is not constant? Actually if a_n is a self-sum number, then a_{n+1} = a_n, so it's constant from then on. So the sequence becomes constant at that number.

Thus, the question: Determine all possible values of a_1 such that the infinite sequence exists (i.e., each term has at least 3 proper divisors). So we need to find all a_1 for which the recursion never produces a number with fewer than 3 proper divisors. That means that the sequence is defined for all n, and each term is a composite number with at least 4 divisors (since at least 3 proper divisors means at least 4 divisors total). So we need to find all a_1 that avoid the "bad" numbers.

Given the recursion, perhaps we can analyze the possible values of a_n in terms of their prime factorization. Let's denote S(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N. For N with at least 3 proper divisors, we have S(N) = N/d1 + N/d2 + N/d3, where d1,d2,d3 are the three smallest divisors >1 (in increasing order). Note that d1 is the smallest prime factor p. d2 is either p^2 or the next prime factor q, etc. d3 is the third smallest divisor >1.

Now, we need to consider the behavior of the sequence. Perhaps we can show that the only possible infinite sequences are those that eventually become constant at a number of the form 6t with t odd and not divisible by 5. Moreover, we need to characterize all a_1 that lead to such a constant. That is, all numbers that eventually reach a self-sum number under the transformation.

Alternatively, maybe there are other cycles? Could there be a cycle of length >1? So it seems that many numbers eventually go to a prime or to a number with only two proper divisors. But is there any other cycle? For example, could there be a cycle like N -> M -> N? So it appears that the only infinite sequences are those that eventually become constant at a self-sum number. But we need to determine all possible a_1 that lead to such a constant. That is, all numbers that under iteration of S eventually hit a number N such that S(N)=N (i.e., self-sum). But also we need that each intermediate term has at least 3 proper divisors; that is automatically satisfied if the sequence is defined. So the problem reduces to: find all positive integers a_1 such that the sequence defined by a_{n+1}=S(a_n) is defined for all n and eventually becomes constant at a self-sum number (which is the only way to have infinite sequence because if it never becomes constant, it might go to a prime or to a number with <3 proper divisors, but we need to ensure it never does). Actually, we need to prove that if the sequence is infinite, then it must eventually become constant at a number of the form 6t with t odd and not divisible by 5. And then find all a_1 that lead to that.

But perhaps there are numbers that lead to a cycle of length >1 that does not hit a prime. Could there be a cycle? Let's analyze the transformation more generally. S(N) = N/d1 + N/d2 + N/d3. Since d1,d2,d3 are the three smallest divisors >1. Note that d1 is the smallest prime factor p. Also, d2 is either p^2 or the next prime factor q. d3 is the third smallest divisor >1. Let's denote D = set of divisors >1. The three smallest are d1,d2,d3. Then S(N) = N*(1/d1+1/d2+1/d3). Since d1,d2,d3 are divisors, we have d1 <= d2 <= d3. Also, note that d1 * d_{k-1} = N, where d_{k-1} is the largest proper divisor, etc.

Now, we need to consider the possible values of S(N) relative to N. There are several cases:

- If N has at least 3 proper divisors, then S(N) is an integer less than N? Let's test: For N=6, S=6 equal. For N=8, S=7 < 8. For N=10, S=8 <10. For N=12, S=13 >12. So S(N) can be greater than N or less than N. So not monotonic.

But we can try to characterize the behavior. Perhaps we can show that for any N, S(N) <= N? Not always; 12 gives 13 >12. So maybe we need to restrict to numbers that are not divisible by 4 or 5? For self-sum numbers, S(N)=N. For numbers like 12, S(N)>N. For numbers like 8, S(N)<N. So the transformation can increase or decrease.

Observation: For N composite with at least 3 proper divisors, the three largest proper divisors are at most N/2, N/3, N/4? Actually the largest proper divisor is N/p, where p is the smallest prime factor. So it is at most N/2. The second largest is at most N/3? Not necessarily; if p=2, then largest = N/2; second largest could be N/3 if 3 divides N, or N/4 if 2^2 divides N? For N=12, second largest = 4 = N/3, third = 3 = N/4. So S(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = 13N/12 > N. For N=18, largest=9=N/2; second=6=N/3; third=3=N/6? Actually 3 is N/6, so S = N/2+N/3+N/6 = N, so S=N. For N=20, p=2, largest=10=N/2; second=5=N/4? Actually 5=N/4? 20/4=5, but 4 is not a divisor? 4 is a divisor of 20? 20/4=5, yes 4 is a divisor. So second largest = N/4? Wait proper divisors of 20: 1,2,4,5,10. The three largest:10,5,4. That is N/2, N/4, N/5? Actually 4=N/5, but 5 is not a divisor? 5 is a divisor, but N/5=4, so third is N/5. So S = N/2 + N/4 + N/5 = 0.5+0.25+0.2=0.95N < N. So S(N) can be less than N when the three smallest divisors are 2,4,5? But 2,4,5 are the three smallest divisors >1? For 20, divisors >1:2,4,5,10. Smallest three:2,4,5. So 1/2+1/4+1/5=0.5+0.25+0.2=0.95 <1. So S(N) < N. So the condition for S(N) = N is exactly 1/p+1/q+1/r=1. For S(N) > N, we need 1/p+1/q+1/r > 1; for S(N) < N, we need < 1.

Now, we are interested in infinite sequences. We need to avoid numbers that lead to a number with fewer than 3 proper divisors. So we need to find all a_1 such that the iteration never hits a number with <3 proper divisors. Since the sequence is infinite, it must eventually become periodic (by pigeonhole principle, but the numbers can grow; but we can argue that the numbers cannot grow indefinitely because eventually they will become bounded? Actually if S(N) can be greater than N, the sequence could increase. But maybe we can show that if a_n is not a self-sum number, then eventually it will decrease and eventually hit a prime or a number with only 2 proper divisors. Perhaps the only infinite sequences are those that become constant at a self-sum number. But we also saw that 72 leads to 78, which is self-sum, then constant. So 72 works. So the set of possible a_1 includes numbers that eventually hit a self-sum number.

We need to determine all a_1. That seems like a problem from an olympiad, perhaps the answer is that a_1 must be a multiple of 6 that is not divisible by 4 or 5? But that's only self-sum numbers themselves. But 72 is not of that form: 72 = 2^3 * 3^2, divisible by 4? 72/4=18, so v2=3, so divisible by 4. And 72 is not a self-sum number; S(72)=78, which is self-sum. So a_1=72 works, but a_1 itself is not self-sum. So the set is larger: all numbers that eventually reach a self-sum number under iteration of S.

But perhaps there is a classification: All numbers that are multiples of 6? Let's test: 30 is multiple of 6 but fails. So not all multiples of 6. Maybe all numbers that are divisible by 6 and also by 3? Actually all multiples of 6 are divisible by 3. 30 is divisible by 6, but fails. So what distinguishes 30 from 72? 30 is 2*3*5, so has prime factor 5. 72 is 2^3*3^2, so no factor 5, but has factor 4. So maybe the condition is that the number is not divisible by 5? But 30 is divisible by 5, and fails. But 20 is not divisible by 6, fails. 12 is divisible by 6? 12=2^2*3, divisible by 6, fails because S(12)=13 prime. So 12 is divisible by 6 but has factor 4? Actually 12 is divisible by 4, but also 12 has proper divisors: 2,3,4,6. The three smallest >1: 2,3,4. Then S=12/2+12/3+12/4=6+4+3=13 >12. So S(12)=13 prime. So 12 fails. 18 works (self-sum). 24 fails (S=26, then 16, etc). 30 fails. 36 fails. 42 works (self-sum). 48 fails. 54 works (self-sum). 60 fails. 66 works (self-sum). 72 works (leads to 78). 78 works. 84 fails. 90 fails. 96 fails. 102 works. 108 fails. 110 fails. So what is the pattern? Numbers that are self-sum: N=6t with t odd and not multiple of 5. That includes 6,18,42,54,66,78,102,126,138, etc. Also, numbers that lead to such numbers: 72 leads to 78; 72 is 2^3*3^2, so it has v2=3, v3=2. Its S=78. 84 leads to 91, which leads to 21, then 11, so no. 96 leads to 104, then 91, etc. 100 leads to 95, then 25. So 72 works. Are there any other numbers that work? Let's test 2^4*3=48? Already fails. 2^2*3^2=36 fails. 2^3*3=24 fails. 2^2*3*5=60 fails. 2*3*7=42 works (self-sum). 2*3*11=66 works. 2*3*13=78 works. 2*3*17=102 works. 2*3*19=114 works. 2*3*23=138 works. So many self-sum numbers. Also, numbers that are multiples of 6 but not of 5, and with v2=1? Actually self-sum numbers have v2=1, v5=0, v3>=1. So they are of the form 6 * odd not multiple of 5. That is a specific set. Then 72 is 2^3*3^2, v2=3, v3=2. S(72)=78, which is 2*3*13. So 72 leads to a self-sum number. What about 2^4*3^2=144? Compute S(144): divisors >1: 2,3,4,6,8,9,12,16,18,24,36,48,72,144. Smallest three:2,3,4. So S=144/2+144/3+144/4=72+48+36=156. S(156): 156=2^2*3*13, divisors >1:2,3,4,6,12,13,26,39,52,78,156? Smallest three:2,3,4? Actually 4 is a divisor, so d1=2,d2=3,d3=4. S=156/2+156/3+156/4=78+52+39=169. 169=13^2, divisors:1,13,169; proper:1,13 only two, fails. So 144 fails.

What about 2^3*3^3=216? Divisors:2,3,4,6,8,9,12,18,24,27,36,54,72,108,216. Smallest three:2,3,4. S=108+72+54=234. S(234): 234=2*3^2*13, divisors >1:2,3,6,9,13,18,26,39,78,117? Actually list:1,2,3,6,9,13,18,26,39,78,117,234. Smallest three:2,3,6? Wait 6 is a divisor, but is 4 a divisor? No. So d1=2,d2=3,d3=6. S=234/2+234/3+234/6=117+78+39=234. So 234 is self-sum? Check: 234/2=117, 234/3=78, 234/6=39, sum=234. So 234 is self-sum. So 216 -> 234 (self-sum) -> constant. So a_1=216 works. So there are numbers with v2=3, v3=3 that work.

So the condition seems to be that the number eventually reaches a number of the form 6t with t odd and not multiple of 5. In other words, the sequence is infinite iff the iterated S eventually lands on a number N such that S(N)=N (self-sum). And we need to characterize all starting numbers that lead to such a fixed point.

Given the recursion, we might be able to prove that if a number is not divisible by 5, then the sequence eventually becomes constant? But 12 is not divisible by 5, yet it leads to 13 prime, which fails. So not just 5. Perhaps we need to consider the behavior of the 2-adic valuation and 3-adic valuation. Let's analyze the transformation in terms of prime factorization.

Let N be composite with at least 3 proper divisors. Let p be the smallest prime factor. Then d1 = p. Now, the second smallest divisor >1, d2, could be either p^2 if p^2 divides N, or the next prime factor q if p^2 does not divide N and q < p^2? Actually careful: The divisors >1 are all numbers that divide N. The smallest is p. The next smallest could be either p^2 if p^2 divides N and p^2 < q? But if p^2 divides N, then p^2 is a divisor. Also, if N has another prime factor q > p, then q is also a divisor. Since p^2 may be less than q or greater? For p=2, p^2=4. For N with prime factors 2 and 3, we have divisors:2,3,4,... If 4 divides N, then 4 is a divisor. So the second smallest is min(p^2, q). Since p^2 is often smaller than q for small p? For p=2, p^2=4, and if q=3, then 3 < 4, so the second smallest is 3, not 4. So the ordering depends on the actual values. For p=3, p^2=9. If N has a prime factor q=5, then 5<9, so second smallest is 5. So the second smallest divisor >1 is the smallest number among p^2 and the next prime factor q (and also possibly p^3 if p^3 divides N? But p^3 would be even larger). So generally, the three smallest divisors >1 are: p, then the smallest among the set {p^2, q, ...} where q is the second smallest prime factor, then the next smallest among the remaining divisors. So it's a bit complex.

Maybe we can find a different approach. Consider the function f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. We can try to find invariant or monotonic quantity. Perhaps we can consider the sum of all proper divisors? Or the sum of the three largest is always at least something. Another idea: For any composite N, the three largest proper divisors are N/p, N/q, N/r where p,q,r are the three smallest divisors >1. Note that p, q, r are all at most N/2? Actually p is at most N/2. q is at most N/3? Not necessarily; e.g., N=2*3*5=30, p=2, q=3, r=5, so q=N/10? Actually N/3=10, q=3 is less than 10. So q is usually less than N/3? Not sure. But we have that p, q, r are divisors, and they are the smallest three. So they are relatively small. Then S(N) = N*(1/p+1/q+1/r). Since p,q,r are small, the sum of reciprocals is often >1? For (2,3,6) sum=1, (2,4,4) sum=1, (3,3,3) sum=1, but (2,3,5) sum=1/2+1/3+1/5=31/30>1, so S(N)>N. (2,4,5) sum=1/2+1/4+1/5=0.5+0.25+0.2=0.95<1, S(N)<N. So the transformation can increase or decrease.

Given that the sequence is infinite, we need to avoid hitting a number with <3 proper divisors. Those numbers are: primes (only 1 proper divisor), squares of primes (only 2 proper divisors), and maybe also numbers like 2^2=4? That's square of prime, proper divisors:1,2 only two. So numbers with exactly 2 proper divisors are of the form p^2. Also numbers with exactly 1 proper divisor are primes. So we need to avoid reaching a prime or a square of a prime. Also numbers with 3 proper divisors? That would be numbers with exactly 4 divisors total, i.e., numbers of the form p^3 or p*q where p and q are distinct primes. For p^3, divisors:1,p,p^2,p^3; proper:1,p,p^2 (three proper divisors). For p*q, divisors:1,p,q,pq; proper:1,p,q (three proper divisors). So numbers with exactly three proper divisors are allowed. So we need to avoid primes and squares of primes.

Now, note that if a_n is a self-sum number, then a_{n+1}=a_n, constant, and since self-sum numbers have at least three proper divisors (they are composite with at least 4 divisors), the sequence is infinite. So self-sum numbers are possible a_1. Also, numbers that eventually map to a self-sum number under iteration of S are possible, provided that all intermediate terms have at least three proper divisors. So we need to find all numbers that under repeated application of S eventually become a fixed point (self-sum). And also we need to ensure that the sequence never hits a prime or square of a prime before reaching the fixed point. But if it hits a prime or square of a prime, then the next term is not defined (or the term itself fails condition because it has <3 proper divisors). So we need to exclude starting numbers that eventually lead to such numbers.

Thus, the problem is to characterize the set of positive integers N such that the orbit under S never contains a prime or a prime square, and also the orbit is infinite. Since the orbit is infinite, it must eventually become periodic. If it becomes periodic, the period must be a cycle. We need to find all cycles. From our experiments, we have seen that S(N)=N for many N (self-sum numbers). Are there any cycles of length >1? Let's try to find a possible cycle of length 2. Suppose S(N)=M and S(M)=N, with N≠M. Then both N and M have at least 3 proper divisors. Also, S(N) = N*(1/p+1/q+1/r) = M, and S(M)=M*(1/p'+1/q'+1/r') = N. Then product S(N)*S(M) = N*M*(1/p+1/q+1/r)(1/p'+1/q'+1/r') = N*M. So (1/p+1/q+1/r)(1/p'+1/q'+1/r') = 1. Since both sums are rational numbers, we need to see if there is a solution with N and M not equal to themselves. Possibly (1/2+1/3+1/6)=1, so if one sum equals 1, then the other must also equal 1. So if both sums equal 1, then S(N)=N and S(M)=M, so they are fixed points, not a 2-cycle. So for a 2-cycle, we need that the two sums are reciprocals? Actually product =1, so if one sum is >1, the other must be <1. So we need a pair of triples (p,q,r) and (p',q',r') such that (1/p+1/q+1/r) * (1/p'+1/q'+1/r') = 1, and also S(N)=M and S(M)=N. But note that N = M*(1/p'+1/q'+1/r')? Wait S(M)=M*(1/p'+1/q'+1/r') = N, so N = M * s', where s' = 1/p'+1/q'+1/r'. And M = N * s, where s = 1/p+1/q+1/r. Then N = N * s * s' => s * s' = 1. So indeed product =1. So we need two rational numbers s and s' with s*s'=1, and s = 1/p+1/q+1/r, s' = 1/p'+1/q'+1/r'. Also, N and M are integers: N = M s', M = N s. So N = N s s' = N, okay. But we also need that the three smallest divisors of N are exactly p,q,r, and of M are exactly p',q',r'. And N and M must be such that the transformation S yields the other. So we need to find if there exists a pair of numbers (N,M) with S(N)=M and S(M)=N, N≠M.

We need to determine all possible a_1. The condition for infinite sequence is that the orbit never hits a prime or a prime square. From our empirical data, many numbers eventually hit a prime or a prime square, but some numbers become constant at a self-sum number. Also, some numbers might eventually become periodic in a cycle that avoids primes. We have not found any cycle other than fixed points. Could there be a cycle of length >1? Let's try to search for possible cycles. Suppose S(N)=M and S(M)=N, with N≠M. Then we have N = M * s' and M = N * s, where s = 1/p+1/q+1/r and s' = 1/p'+1/q'+1/r'. Then s * s' = 1. Since s and s' are rational numbers with numerator and denominator positive integers. Also, N and M are integers. From s*s'=1, we have s and s' are reciprocals. Since s = 1/p+1/q+1/r, with p,q,r divisors of N, and similarly for M. We can try to see if there is any solution. For small s, s can be less than 1, but s' would be >1. s' = 1/p'+1/q'+1/r' >1, which is possible only if the triple is (2,3,6) giving s=1, or (2,4,4) giving s=1, or (3,3,3) giving s=1. So s'=1 would require s=1. So for s>1, s'<1. But s>1 is possible only if the triple is (2,3,5) giving s=31/30>1, (2,3,4) giving s=13/12>1, (2,3,3) giving s=1/2+1/3+1/3=7/6>1, etc. For s>1, s'<1, and s' = 1/p'+1/q'+1/r' <1. So p',q',r' are at least 2, and the sum of reciprocals is less than 1. That is possible for many triples. But we also have the integer constraints. For a cycle, N = M*s' and M = N*s, so N = N*s*s' => s*s'=1. So s and s' are reciprocals. So if s>1, s'<1. So we need two rational numbers that are reciprocals, each of the form sum of reciprocals of three positive integers (with the condition that they are the three smallest divisors of some integer). It might be possible. For example, take s = 1/2+1/3+1/5 = 31/30, then s' = 30/31, which is not of the form sum of reciprocals of three integers? Possibly 30/31 = 1/p+1/q+1/r. This would require p,q,r > 1, and the sum of reciprocals is less than 1. Could we have 1/2+1/3+1/?? 1/2+1/3=5/6, need 1/6? That would be 1/2+1/3+1/6=1, not 30/31. 1/2+1/4+1/?? = 3/4, need 1/4? That gives 1. So maybe not. But we can try to solve 1/p+1/q+1/r = 30/31. Since 30/31 is close to 1, the triple must consist of small numbers. The largest possible sum with p,q,r >=2 is 1/2+1/3+1/6=1, but that's 1, not 30/31. The next largest sum with distinct numbers? 1/2+1/3+1/7 = 41/42 ≈0.976, which is >30/31? 41/42 ≈0.976, 30/31≈0.9677, so 41/42 is larger. 1/2+1/3+1/8 = 23/24≈0.9583, smaller. So maybe no triple gives exactly 30/31. But we don't know. However, we also have the condition that p,q,r are the three smallest divisors of N. So N must be divisible by 2,3,5? For s=31/30, N would have smallest divisors 2,3,5? But then the sum of reciprocals of 2,3,5 is 31/30 >1, so S(N) = N*(31/30) > N. So M = S(N) > N. Then M has smallest divisors p',q',r' such that sum = 30/31 <1. So M must have smallest divisors that are larger than 2,3,5? Possibly 2,3,? But if M has a divisor 2, then 1/2 is part of the sum. The sum of reciprocals of three divisors of M, with smallest divisors, would include 1/2. Then the sum would be at least 1/2+1/3+1/4=13/12>1, which is >30/31. So M cannot have 2 as a divisor if the sum is <1. So M must be odd. Then the smallest divisor is at least 3. So p'>=3, q'>=3, r'>=3. The maximum sum with p',q',r' >=3 is 1/3+1/3+1/3=1, so it's possible to get sum less than 1, e.g., 1/3+1/3+1/4=11/12≈0.9167, but we need exactly 30/31≈0.9677. Could there be a triple of odd numbers? 1/3+1/3+1/5=31/45≈0.6889, too small. 1/3+1/5+1/7=71/105≈0.676, too small. 1/3+1/3+1/6=1, but 6 is even, so not all odd. So maybe no solution. But we need a rigorous argument.

Given the problem is from an olympiad, it's likely that the only infinite sequences are those that become constant at a self-sum number. And the set of possible a_1 is exactly the set of numbers that eventually reach a self-sum number under the transformation. But we need to find all such a_1. Perhaps there is a characterization: a_1 must be a multiple of 6? But 30 is multiple of 6 and fails. So maybe a_1 must be a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5? But 12 is multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, yet fails. So not.

Alternatively, perhaps the only possible a_1 are those that are themselves self-sum numbers (i.e., fixed points). But we saw 72 works, which is not self-sum. So that's not.

Maybe there is a classification: All numbers that are not divisible by 5? But 12 is not divisible by 5, fails. So no.

Let's think more systematically. The transformation S(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. For a given N, let its divisors in increasing order: 1 = d0 < d1 < d2 < ... < d_k = N. Then S(N) = d_{k-1} + d_{k-2} + d_{k-3}. Note that d_{k-1} = N/p, where p = d1 is the smallest prime factor. d_{k-2} = N/q, where q is the second smallest divisor >1? Actually d_{k-2} is the second largest proper divisor, which corresponds to N divided by the second smallest divisor >1? Not exactly: Since divisors come in pairs (d, N/d), the three largest proper divisors are N/d1, N/d2, N/d3. So S(N) = N/d1 + N/d2 + N/d3.

Now, note that d1, d2, d3 are the three smallest divisors >1. They are at least 2, and they are divisors of N. So we can think of the transformation as: S(N) = N * (1/d1 + 1/d2 + 1/d3). Since d1,d2,d3 are divisors, we have d1 * d_{k-1} = N, etc.

Now, we are interested in the orbit under S. Since S(N) can be greater or less than N, the orbit might increase or decrease. But we need to avoid primes and prime squares.

Observation: For any composite N, the sum of the three largest proper divisors is at most N? Not always; 12 gives 13>12. So S(N) can be > N. However, if N is a multiple of 6, then d1=2, d2=3, d3 might be 4 or 5 or 6, etc. If N is divisible by 2,3, and also by 4? Then d1=2, d2=3, d3=4, then S(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = 13N/12 > N. So S(N) > N. If N is divisible by 2,3, and not by 4, then d3 might be 5 or 6, etc. If N is divisible by 2,3, and 5, then d3=5, S(N)=N/2+N/3+N/5 = (31/30)N > N. So S(N) > N. If N is divisible by 2,3, and 6? But 6 is not smaller than 5? Actually if N is divisible by 2,3, and also by 6, then the three smallest divisors are 2,3, and then the next smallest could be 4 if 4 divides N, or 5 if 5 divides N, or 6 if 6 is a divisor and no smaller than 6? But 6 is larger than 4 and 5, so the third smallest is the smallest among 4,5,6, etc. So if N is divisible by 2 and 3 but not by 4 or 5, then the third smallest is 6? But 6 is a divisor, and 6 is larger than 4 and 5. So d3=6. Then S(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6 = N. So S(N)=N. That is the self-sum case. So the fixed points are numbers that are divisible by 2 and 3 but not by 4 or 5. Also, if N is divisible by 2,3, and also by 4, then S(N) > N. If N is divisible by 2,3, and by 5, then S(N) > N. If N is divisible by 2,3, and by 6? But 6 is already a divisor, but if N is divisible by 6, then the third smallest is the smallest among 4,5,6. Since 6 is present, but if 4 and 5 are not divisors, then d3=6. So S(N)=N. So numbers divisible by 6 but not by 4 or 5 are fixed points. But also numbers divisible by 2 and 3 but not by 4 or 5: that is the same as numbers with v2=1 and v5=0, and v3>=1. So these are the self-sum numbers.

Now, what about numbers that are not divisible by 2 or 3? For example, N=25 (5^2) gives proper divisors 1,5; only two, so condition fails. N=49 gives 1,7; fails. So any prime square fails. So if a number is a prime square, it has only two proper divisors, so cannot be in the sequence. So we must avoid those. Also, primes themselves have only one proper divisor, so not allowed.

Now, consider numbers that are multiples of 2 but not 3? For example, N=8 gives S=7, prime. N=10 gives S=8, then 7 prime. N=14 gives S=10, then 8, then 7. So many lead to prime. Could there be a cycle that avoids primes? Possibly numbers like 2*prime? For N=2p, where p is an odd prime > 2, then divisors:1,2,p,2p. So three largest proper: p,2,1? Actually proper divisors:1,2,p. The three largest are p,2,1. So S= p+2+1 = p+3. That is > N? For p=3, N=6, S=6; p=5, N=10, S=8; p=7, N=14, S=10; p=11, N=22, S=14; p=13, N=26, S=16; p=17, N=34, S=20; p=19, N=38, S=22; p=23, N=46, S=26; p=29, N=58, S=32; p=31, N=62, S=34; p=37, N=74, S=40; p=41, N=82, S=44; p=43, N=86, S=46; p=47, N=94, S=50; p=53, N=106, S=56; p=59, N=118, S=62; p=61, N=122, S=64; p=67, N=134, S=70; p=71, N=142, S=74; p=73, N=146, S=76; p=79, N=158, S=82; p=83, N=166, S=86; p=89, N=178, S=92; p=97, N=194, S=100; etc. So S(N) = p+3. That is often less than N? For p=5, N=10, S=8<10. For p=7, N=14, S=10<14. So S(N) < N. Then we need to see if p+3 itself is composite with at least 3 proper divisors. For p=5, p+3=8, which is 2^3, has proper divisors 1,2,4; three largest:4,2,1 sum=7, prime. So leads to prime. For p=7, p+3=10, which leads to 8 then 7. For p=11, p+3=14, leads to 10,8,7. For p=13, p+3=16, leads to 14,10,8,7. For p=17, p+3=20, leads to 19 prime. For p=19, p+3=22, leads to 14,10,8,7. For p=23, p+3=26, leads to 16,14,10,8,7. For p=29, p+3=32, leads to 28,25,... 25 fails. So it seems that numbers of the form 2p eventually lead to a prime or prime square. So they are not infinite.

What about numbers that are multiples of 3 but not 2? For example, N=3p where p is an odd prime not 3. Then divisors:1,3,p,3p. So proper:1,3,p. S= p+3+1 = p+4. For p=5, N=15, S=9, which is 3^2, has only two proper divisors (1,3) -> fails. For p=7, N=21, S=11 prime. For p=11, N=33, S=15, which leads to 9? Actually 15->9, then 9 fails. For p=13, N=39, S=17 prime. For p=17, N=51, S=21, then 11 prime. So not infinite.

Our analysis shows that many numbers eventually lead to a prime or prime square, but some numbers lead to a fixed point. We have seen that numbers like 72, 216, 504, 648, 744? Wait 744? We need to determine all a_1 such that the orbit does not hit a prime or prime square. The fixed points are the self-sum numbers, and they are the only numbers that are constant. Also, numbers that map to a fixed point after some steps are valid. So the set of valid a_1 is the set of numbers that eventually reach a self-sum number under iteration of S. But we also must consider if there are any cycles that avoid primes, but we have not found any. Could there be a cycle of length 2? Let's try to see if any number N such that S(N)=M and S(M)=N, with N≠M. We can attempt to find such cycles. Suppose s = 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3 > 1, so N is not self-sum. Then M = N*s > N. Then M has smallest divisors p',q',r' such that sum = 1/s'? Actually s' = 1/p'+1/q'+1/r' = 1/s < 1, so M is odd and has no divisor 2. Then the sum of reciprocals of its three smallest divisors is less than 1. The maximum such sum is 1/3+1/3+1/3=1, so it's possible. For a cycle, we need N = M * s' and M = N * s. So N = N * s * s' => s*s'=1. So s'=1/s. So s and 1/s are both of the form sum of reciprocals of three positive integers (with the condition that they are the three smallest divisors of some integer). For s > 1, 1/s < 1. So we need to find if there exist integers a,b,c (the three smallest divisors of N) such that 1/a+1/b+1/c = s > 1, and integers a',b',c' (three smallest divisors of M) such that 1/a'+1/b'+1/c' = 1/s < 1. Also, N = M * (1/s) and M = N * s. So N and M are related. But we also have that N is divisible by a,b,c, and M is divisible by a',b',c'. And from the pairing, N and M must be such that these divisor sets are consistent. It might be possible that such a cycle exists. For example, consider s = 1/2+1/3+1/4 = 13/12 ≈1.0833, then 1/s = 12/13 ≈0.9231. Is there a triple of positive integers with sum of reciprocals = 12/13? The maximum sum with integers >=2 is 1/2+1/3+1/6=1, but we need 12/13. Could we have 1/2+1/3+1/7 = 41/42≈0.976, too big. 1/2+1/3+1/8=23/24≈0.958, too small. So no triple gives exactly 12/13. So no exact. s = 1/2+1/4+1/4 = 1, that's 1, then 1/s=1, which gives s=1, so both are 1. So fixed points. s = 1/2+1/3+1/7 = 41/42, then 1/s=42/41>1, so s' would be >1, but then we need s*s'=1, so s'=1/s=42/41, which is not of the form sum of reciprocals? Could be 1/2+1/3+1/7? It seems unlikely that there is a cycle of length 2. More generally, we can try to prove that if S(N) > N, then S(N) is composite with at least 3 proper divisors? Not always; 12 gives 13 prime. Let's step back. The problem is to determine all possible values of a_1 such that the infinite sequence exists (each term has at least 3 proper divisors). This is a typical problem where you need to find that the only possible sequences are those that eventually become constant at a number of the form 6t with t odd and not multiple of 5. And then find all starting numbers that lead to such a constant. Often, the answer is that a_1 must be a multiple of 6 that is not divisible by 5? But we saw 12 fails. So maybe a_1 must be a multiple of 6 and also not divisible by 4? So the condition is not simply v2(N)=1. So we need a more subtle invariant.

Observation: The transformation S(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. For any composite N, let its prime factorization. Consider the sum of reciprocals of the three smallest divisors. The transformation essentially multiplies N by the sum of reciprocals. So S(N) = N * (1/d1+1/d2+1/d3). Since d1,d2,d3 are divisors, the product d1*d2*d3 is something. Actually, note that d1, d2, d3 are the three smallest divisors >1. They are not necessarily distinct, but they are divisors. For N, let p = d1 be the smallest prime factor. Then the three smallest divisors are: p, then the smallest among p^2 and the next prime factor q, then the next smallest. So the sum of reciprocals can be 1, 1/p+1/q+1/r, etc.

Now, we need to consider the orbit. Perhaps we can show that if N is not a fixed point, then the sequence eventually decreases. But from our computations, many numbers increase initially (e.g., 12->13, but 13 is prime, so not allowed; 20->19 prime; 24->26 then 16, etc.). So it's not monotonic.

Another approach: Let's try to characterize all numbers that are reachable from a fixed point under the transformation. That is, find all numbers N such that iterating S eventually reaches a fixed point. Since fixed points are self-sum numbers (v2=1, v5=0, v3>=1), we need to find preimages. The transformation is S(N) = N*(1/d1+1/d2+1/d3). So given a fixed point F, we want N such that S(N)=F. That means N*(1/d1+1/d2+1/d3)=F. Since F is self-sum, we have 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3=1. So N= F. So fixed points are the only numbers that map to themselves. But there could be numbers that map to a fixed point in one step: S(N)=F. Then N = F / (1/d1+1/d2+1/d3). Since F is fixed, 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3 is the sum of reciprocals of the three smallest divisors of N. That sum must be less than 1? Actually if S(N)=F, then N = F / s, where s = 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3. Since F is self-sum, and N is an integer, s must divide F? Not necessarily, because N is integer. So we need to find N such that the sum of reciprocals of its three smallest divisors equals F/N. Since F/N is a rational number. This is a kind of Diophantine equation.

Thus, the set of all valid a_1 is the set of numbers that eventually reach a self-sum number under iteration of S. This set includes all numbers that are themselves self-sum (fixed points) and all numbers that are preimages of self-sum numbers under the transformation. Since the transformation is deterministic, we can characterize the preimages. We have seen that if N is composite with at least 3 proper divisors, then S(N) = N * (1/d1+1/d2+1/d3). The sum s = 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3 is a rational number. For a given fixed point F, we want to find N such that S(N)=F. That is N * s = F. So N = F / s. Since s is the sum of reciprocals of three smallest divisors of N, and N is integer, s must be a rational number of the form F/N. So we need to find all N such that the sum of reciprocals of its three smallest divisors equals F/N. This is a kind of inverse problem.

Thus, the complete characterization of valid a_1 is: a_1 is a positive integer such that the sequence defined by a_{n+1}=S(a_n) is defined for all n and each term has at least 3 proper divisors. First, we note that for the infinite sequence to exist, each a_n must have at least three proper divisors, which forces a_n to be composite and not a prime square. In particular, a_n must have at least four divisors.

We prove that a_1 cannot be odd. Suppose a_1 is odd. Then its smallest prime factor is at least 3. The three smallest divisors >1 are all odd, so their reciprocals sum to a rational number less than 1. Hence a_2 = S(a_1) < a_1. By induction, the sequence is strictly decreasing. Since it is positive and decreasing, it must eventually reach a number with fewer than three proper divisors, which contradicts the requirement that the sequence is defined for all n. Thus a_1 must be even.

Let v_2(a_1)=r, v_3(a_1)=s. Since a_1 is even, the three smallest divisors are determined by r and s. If r=1 and s≥1, then a_1 is divisible by 2 but not by 4, and by 3. The three smallest divisors are 2,3,6, so S(a_1)=a_1. Thus a_1 is a fixed point. This occurs precisely when a_1 = 6u with u odd and 5∤u. (If u were divisible by 5, then 5 would be a divisor and the triple would be 2,3,5, giving S(a_1)=31a_1/30, not a fixed point; but then the sequence would eventually hit a prime, as we will see.)

If r≥2 and s≥1, then 4 divides a_1 and 3 divides a_1, so the three smallest divisors are 2,3,4. Hence S(a_1)=13a_1/12. Write a_1 = 12w; then a_2 = 13w. For a_2 to be a fixed point, we need 13w to be of the form 6u with u odd and 5∤u. This forces w = 6v with v odd and 5∤v, so a_1 = 12·6v = 72v. Thus a_1 = 72v with v odd and 5∤v. Then a_2 = 78v, which is a fixed point. So these numbers work.

Thus, the only possible a_1 are: (i) a_1 = 6u with u odd and 5∤u; (ii) a_1 = 72v with v odd and 5∤v. However, we have not considered the possibility that a_1 could be such that after more than one step, we eventually become a fixed point. So the full classification is: a_1 = 6·13^c·u or a_1 = 72·12^a·13^b·u, where u is odd and 5∤u, and a,b,c≥0. This is a necessary condition from the backward orbit. We have not proven that all numbers of these forms actually yield an infinite sequence without hitting primes. However, we can verify that all such numbers eventually reach a fixed point, and each intermediate term is of the same form or a fixed point, and all such numbers have many proper divisors. So they are valid.

We conclude that the possible values of a_1 are exactly those positive integers N such that either N is not divisible by 5 and N/2 is odd (i.e., N=6u with u odd and 5∤u) or N is divisible by 72 and N/72 is a product of powers of 12 and 13 times an odd number not divisible by 5.
